			</ul>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Discrete model studied in computer science</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Frame"><a href="/wiki/File:Gospers_glider_gun.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Gospers_glider_gun.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="180" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="180" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bill_Gosper" title="Bill Gosper">Gosper's</a> <a href="/wiki/Gun_(cellular_automaton)" title="Gun (cellular automaton)">Glider Gun</a> creating "<a href="/wiki/Glider_(Conway%27s_Life)" class="mw-redirect" title="Glider (Conway&#39;s Life)">gliders</a>" in the cellular automaton <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Conway's Game of Life</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>A <b>cellular automaton</b> (pl. <b>cellular automata</b>, abbrev. <b>CA</b>) is a discrete <a href="/wiki/Model_of_computation" title="Model of computation">model of computation</a> studied in <a href="/wiki/Automata_theory" title="Automata theory">automata theory</a>. Cellular automata are also called <b>cellular spaces</b>, <b>tessellation automata</b>, <b>homogeneous structures</b>, <b>cellular structures</b>, <b>tessellation structures</b>, and <b>iterative arrays</b>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-reviews&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reviews-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cellular automata have found application in various areas, including <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_biology" class="mw-redirect" title="Theoretical biology">theoretical biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microstructure" title="Microstructure">microstructure</a> modeling.
</p><p>A cellular automaton consists of a regular grid of <i>cells</i>, each in one of a finite number of <i><a href="/wiki/State_(computer_science)" title="State (computer science)">states</a></i>, such as <i>on</i> and <i>off</i> (in contrast to a <a href="/wiki/Coupled_map_lattice" title="Coupled map lattice">coupled map lattice</a>). The grid can be in any finite number of dimensions. For each cell, a set of cells called its <i>neighborhood</i> is defined relative to the specified cell. An initial state (time <i>t</i>&#160;=&#160;0) is selected by assigning a state for each cell. A new <i>generation</i> is created (advancing <i>t</i> by 1), according to some fixed <i>rule</i> (generally, a mathematical function)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that determines the new state of each cell in terms of the current state of the cell and the states of the cells in its neighborhood. Typically, the rule for updating the state of cells is the same for each cell and does not change over time, and is applied to the whole grid simultaneously,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though exceptions are known, such as the <a href="/wiki/Stochastic_cellular_automaton" title="Stochastic cellular automaton">stochastic cellular automaton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asynchronous_cellular_automaton" title="Asynchronous cellular automaton">asynchronous cellular automaton</a>.
</p><p>The concept was originally conceived in the 1940s by <a href="/wiki/Stanislaw_Ulam" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanislaw Ulam">Stanislaw Ulam</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a> while they were contemporaries at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a>. While studied by some throughout the 1950s and 1960s, it was not until the 1970s and <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Conway's Game of Life</a>, a two-dimensional cellular automaton, that interest in the subject expanded beyond academia. In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Wolfram" title="Stephen Wolfram">Stephen Wolfram</a> engaged in a systematic study of one-dimensional cellular automata, or what he calls <a href="/wiki/Elementary_cellular_automaton" title="Elementary cellular automaton">elementary cellular automata</a>; his research assistant <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Cook" title="Matthew Cook">Matthew Cook</a> showed that <a href="/wiki/Rule_110" title="Rule 110">one of these rules</a> is <a href="/wiki/Turing-complete" class="mw-redirect" title="Turing-complete">Turing-complete</a>.
</p><p>The primary classifications of cellular automata, as outlined by Wolfram, are numbered one to four. They are, in order, automata in which patterns generally stabilize into <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity" class="mw-redirect" title="Homogeneity">homogeneity</a>, automata in which patterns evolve into mostly stable or oscillating structures, automata in which patterns evolve in a seemingly chaotic fashion, and automata in which patterns become extremely complex and may last for a long time, with stable local structures.  This last class is thought to be <a href="/wiki/Computationally_universal" class="mw-redirect" title="Computationally universal">computationally universal</a>, or capable of simulating a <a href="/wiki/Turing_machine" title="Turing machine">Turing machine</a>. Special types of cellular automata are <i><a href="/wiki/Reversible_cellular_automaton" title="Reversible cellular automaton">reversible</a></i>, where only a single configuration leads directly to a subsequent one, and <i>totalistic</i>, in which the future value of individual cells only depends on the total value of a group of neighboring cells. Cellular automata can simulate a variety of real-world systems, including biological and chemical ones.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner span:not(.skin-invert-image):not(.skin-invert):not(.bg-transparent) img{background-color:white}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner span:not(.skin-invert-image):not(.skin-invert):not(.bg-transparent) img{background-color:white}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:CA-Moore.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/CA-Moore.svg/250px-CA-Moore.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/CA-Moore.svg/330px-CA-Moore.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/CA-Moore.svg/500px-CA-Moore.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">The red cells are the <a href="/wiki/Moore_neighborhood" title="Moore neighborhood">Moore neighborhood</a> for the blue cell.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:CA-von-Neumann.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/CA-von-Neumann.svg/250px-CA-von-Neumann.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/CA-von-Neumann.svg/330px-CA-von-Neumann.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/CA-von-Neumann.svg/500px-CA-von-Neumann.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">The red cells are the <a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_neighborhood" title="Von Neumann neighborhood">von Neumann neighborhood</a> for the blue cell. The range-2 "cross neighborhood" includes the pink cells as well.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>One way to simulate a two-dimensional cellular automaton is with an infinite sheet of <a href="/wiki/Graph_paper" title="Graph paper">graph paper</a> along with a set of rules for the cells to follow. Each square is called a "cell" and each cell has two possible states, black or white. The <i>neighborhood</i> of a cell is the nearby, usually adjacent, cells. The two most common types of neighborhoods are the <i><a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_neighborhood" title="Von Neumann neighborhood">von Neumann neighborhood</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Moore_neighborhood" title="Moore neighborhood">Moore neighborhood</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kier-seybold-cheng-15&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kier-seybold-cheng-15-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The former, named after the founding cellular automaton theorist, consists of the four <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthogonal">orthogonally</a> adjacent cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kier-seybold-cheng-15&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kier-seybold-cheng-15-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The latter includes the von Neumann neighborhood as well as the four diagonally adjacent cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kier-seybold-cheng-15&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kier-seybold-cheng-15-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For such a cell and its Moore neighborhood, there are 512 (= 2<sup>9</sup>) possible patterns. For each of the 512 possible patterns, the rule table would state whether the center cell will be black or white on the next time interval. <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Conway's Game of Life</a> is a popular version of this model. Another common neighborhood type is the <i>extended von Neumann neighborhood</i>, which includes the two closest cells in each orthogonal direction, for a total of eight.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kier-seybold-cheng-15&#95;5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kier-seybold-cheng-15-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The general equation for the total number of automata possible is <i>k</i><sup><i>k</i><sup><i>s</i></sup></sup>, where <i>k</i> is the number of possible states for a cell, and <i>s</i> is the number of neighboring cells (including the cell to be calculated itself) used to determine the cell's next state.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bialynick-9&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bialynick-9-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, in the two-dimensional system with a Moore neighborhood, the total number of automata possible would be 2<sup>2<sup>9</sup></sup>, or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7154134000000000000♠"></span>1.34<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>154</sup></span>.
</p><p>It is usually assumed that every cell in the universe starts in the same state, except for a finite number of cells in other states; the assignment of state values is called a <i>configuration</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-41&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-41-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More generally, it is sometimes assumed that the universe starts out covered with a periodic pattern, and only a finite number of cells violate that pattern. The latter assumption is common in one-dimensional cellular automata.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Torus.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Torus.png/250px-Torus.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Torus.png/500px-Torus.png 1.5x" data-file-width="784" data-file-height="502" /></a><figcaption>A toroidal shape (the <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a>)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Cellular automata are often simulated on a finite grid rather than an infinite one. In two dimensions, the universe would be a rectangle instead of an infinite plane. The obvious problem with finite grids is how to handle the cells on the edges. How they are handled will affect the values of all the cells in the grid. One possible method is to allow the values in those cells to remain constant. Another method is to define neighborhoods differently for these cells. One could say that they have fewer neighbors, but then one would also have to define new rules for the cells located on the edges. These cells are usually handled with <a href="/wiki/Periodic_boundary_condition" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodic boundary condition">periodic boundary conditions</a> resulting in a <i>toroidal</i> arrangement: when one goes off the top, one comes in at the corresponding position on the bottom, and when one goes off the left, one comes in on the right. (This essentially simulates an infinite periodic tiling, and in the field of <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial differential equations">partial differential equations</a> is sometimes referred to as <i>periodic</i> boundary conditions.) This can be visualized as taping the left and right edges of the rectangle to form a tube, then taping the top and bottom edges of the tube to form a <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a> (doughnut shape). Universes of other <a href="/wiki/Dimensions" class="mw-redirect" title="Dimensions">dimensions</a> are handled similarly. This solves boundary problems with neighborhoods, but another advantage is that it is easily programmable using <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">modular arithmetic</a> functions. For example, in a 1-dimensional cellular automaton like the examples below, the neighborhood of a cell <i>x<sub>i</sub><sup>t</sup></i> is {<i>x</i><sub><i>i</i>−1</sub><sup style="margin-left:-2ex;"><i>t</i>−1</sup>, <i>x</i><sub><i>i</i></sub><sup><i>t</i>−1</sup>, <i>x</i><sub><i>i</i>+1</sub><sup style="margin-left:-2ex;"><i>t</i>−1</sup>}, where <i>t</i> is the time step (vertical), and <i>i</i> is the index (horizontal) in one generation.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Ulam" title="Stanisław Ulam">Stanisław Ulam</a>, while working at the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> in the 1940s, studied the growth of crystals, using a simple <a href="/wiki/Lattice_model_(physics)" title="Lattice model (physics)">lattice network</a> as his model.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pickover&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pickover-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the same time, <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a> — Ulam's colleague at Los Alamos — was working on the problem of <a href="/wiki/Self-replicating_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-replicating system">self-replicating systems</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-1&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-1-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Von Neumann's initial design was founded upon the notion of one robot building another robot. This design is known as the kinematic model.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As he developed this design, von Neumann came to realize the great difficulty of building a self-replicating robot, and of the great cost in providing the robot with a "sea of parts" from which to build its replicant. Neumann wrote a paper entitled "The general and logical theory of automata" for the <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_A._Jeffress#The_Hixon_Symposium" title="Lloyd A. Jeffress">Hixon Symposium</a> in 1948.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-1&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-1-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ulam was the one who suggested using a <i>discrete</i> system for creating a reductionist model of self-replication.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-3&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-3-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-xxix&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-xxix-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nils_Aall_Barricelli" title="Nils Aall Barricelli">Nils Aall Barricelli</a> performed many of the earliest explorations of these models of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">artificial life</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vonneumann-john_r.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Vonneumann-john_r.jpg/250px-Vonneumann-john_r.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="331" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Vonneumann-john_r.jpg/500px-Vonneumann-john_r.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3274" data-file-height="4341" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos</a> ID badge</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ulam and von Neumann created a method for calculating liquid motion in the late 1950s. The driving concept of the method was to consider a liquid as a group of discrete units and calculate the motion of each based on its neighbors' behaviors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bialynick-8&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bialynick-8-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus was born the first system of cellular automata. Like Ulam's lattice network, <a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_cellular_automata" class="mw-redirect" title="Von Neumann cellular automata">von Neumann's cellular automata</a> are two-dimensional, with his self-replicator implemented algorithmically. The result was a <a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_universal_constructor" title="Von Neumann universal constructor">universal copier and constructor</a> working within a cellular automaton with a small neighborhood (only those cells that touch are neighbors; for von Neumann's cellular automata, only <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> cells), and with 29 states per cell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-876&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-876-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Von Neumann gave an <a href="/wiki/Constructive_proof" title="Constructive proof">existence proof</a> that a particular pattern would make endless copies of itself within the given cellular universe by designing a 200,000 cell configuration that could do so.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-876&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-876-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This design is known as the <a href="/wiki/Tessellation" title="Tessellation">tessellation</a> model, and is called a <a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_universal_constructor" title="Von Neumann universal constructor">von Neumann universal constructor</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-TSRA&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TSRA-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Also in the 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Rosenblueth" title="Arturo Rosenblueth">Arturo Rosenblueth</a> developed a model of excitable media with some of the characteristics of a cellular automaton.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wiener-Rosenblueth&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiener-Rosenblueth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their specific motivation was the mathematical description of impulse conduction in cardiac systems. However their model is not a cellular automaton because the medium in which signals propagate is continuous, and wave fronts are curves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wiener-Rosenblueth&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiener-Rosenblueth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-reshodko&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reshodko-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A true cellular automaton model of excitable media was developed and studied by J. M. Greenberg and S. P. Hastings in 1978; see <a href="/wiki/Greenberg-Hastings_cellular_automaton" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenberg-Hastings cellular automaton">Greenberg-Hastings cellular automaton</a>. The original work of Wiener and Rosenblueth contains many insights and continues to be cited in modern research publications on <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">cardiac arrhythmia</a> and excitable systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s, cellular automata were studied as a particular type of <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical system</a> and the connection with the mathematical field of <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_dynamics" title="Symbolic dynamics">symbolic dynamics</a> was established for the first time. In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_A._Hedlund" title="Gustav A. Hedlund">Gustav A. Hedlund</a> compiled many results following this point of view<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in what is still considered as a seminal paper for the mathematical study of cellular automata. The most fundamental result is the characterization in the <a href="/wiki/Curtis%E2%80%93Hedlund%E2%80%93Lyndon_theorem" title="Curtis–Hedlund–Lyndon theorem">Curtis–Hedlund–Lyndon theorem</a> of the set of global rules of cellular automata as the set of <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous</a> <a href="/wiki/Endomorphisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Endomorphisms">endomorphisms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shift_space" title="Shift space">shift spaces</a>.
</p><p>In 1969, German computer pioneer <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Zuse" title="Konrad Zuse">Konrad Zuse</a> published his book <i><a href="/wiki/Calculating_Space" title="Calculating Space">Calculating Space</a></i>, proposing that the physical laws of the universe are discrete by nature, and that the entire universe is the output of a deterministic computation on a single cellular automaton; "Zuse's Theory" became the foundation of the field of study called <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_physics" title="Digital physics">digital physics</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-182&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-182-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1969 computer scientist <a href="/wiki/Alvy_Ray_Smith" title="Alvy Ray Smith">Alvy Ray Smith</a> completed a Stanford PhD dissertation on Cellular Automata Theory, the first mathematical treatment of CA as a general class of computers. Many papers came from this dissertation: He showed the equivalence of neighborhoods of various shapes, how to reduce a Moore to a von Neumann neighborhood or how to reduce any neighborhood to a von Neumann neighborhood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">proved</a> that two-dimensional CA are computation universal, introduced 1-dimensional CA, and showed that they too are computation universal, even with simple neighborhoods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He showed how to subsume the complex von Neumann proof of construction universality (and hence self-reproducing machines) into a consequence of computation universality in a 1-dimensional CA.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Intended as the introduction to the German edition of von Neumann's book on CA, he wrote a survey of the field with dozens of references to papers, by many authors in many countries over a decade or so of work, often overlooked by modern CA researchers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s a two-state, two-dimensional cellular automaton named <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Game of Life</a> became widely known, particularly among the early computing community. Invented by <a href="/wiki/John_Horton_Conway" title="John Horton Conway">John Conway</a> and popularized by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Gardner" title="Martin Gardner">Martin Gardner</a> in a <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i> article,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> its rules are as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>Any live cell with fewer than two live neighbours dies, as if caused by underpopulation.</li>
<li>Any live cell with two or three live neighbours lives on to the next generation.</li>
<li>Any live cell with more than three live neighbours dies, as if by overpopulation.</li>
<li>Any dead cell with exactly three live neighbours becomes a live cell, as if by reproduction.</li></ol>
<p>Despite its simplicity, the system achieves an impressive diversity of behavior, fluctuating between apparent <a href="/wiki/Randomness" title="Randomness">randomness</a> and order. One of the most apparent features of the Game of Life is the frequent occurrence of <i>gliders</i>, arrangements of cells that essentially move themselves across the grid. It is possible to arrange the automaton so that the gliders interact to perform computations, and after much effort it has been shown that the Game of Life can emulate a <a href="/wiki/Universal_Turing_machine" title="Universal Turing machine">universal Turing machine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was viewed as a largely recreational topic, and little follow-up work was done outside of investigating the particularities of the Game of Life and a few related rules in the early 1970s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wainwright-16&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wainwright-16-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Wolfram" title="Stephen Wolfram">Stephen Wolfram</a> independently began working on cellular automata in mid-1981 after considering how complex patterns seemed formed in nature in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-880&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-880-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His investigations were initially spurred by a desire to model systems such as the <a href="/wiki/Neural_network_(biology)" title="Neural network (biology)">neural networks</a> found in brains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-880&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-880-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He published his first paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Reviews_of_Modern_Physics" title="Reviews of Modern Physics">Reviews of Modern Physics</a></i> investigating <i><a href="/wiki/Elementary_cellular_automaton" title="Elementary cellular automaton">elementary cellular automata</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Rule_30" title="Rule 30">Rule 30</a> in particular) in June 1983.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-reviews&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reviews-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-880&#95;29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-880-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The unexpected complexity of the behavior of these simple rules led Wolfram to suspect that complexity in nature may be due to similar mechanisms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-880&#95;29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-880-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His investigations, however, led him to realize that cellular automata were poor at modelling neural networks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-880&#95;29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-880-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, during this period Wolfram formulated the concepts of intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Randomness" title="Randomness">randomness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_irreducibility" title="Computational irreducibility">computational irreducibility</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-881&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-881-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and suggested that <a href="/wiki/Rule_110" title="Rule 110">Rule 110</a> may be <a href="/wiki/Turing-complete" class="mw-redirect" title="Turing-complete">universal</a>—a fact proved later by Wolfram's research assistant <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Cook" title="Matthew Cook">Matthew Cook</a> in the 1990s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Classification">Classification</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Wolfram, in <i><a href="/wiki/A_New_Kind_of_Science" title="A New Kind of Science">A New Kind of Science</a></i> and several papers dating from the mid-1980s, defined four classes into which cellular automata and several other simple computational models can be divided depending on their behavior. While earlier studies in cellular automata tended to try to identify types of patterns for specific rules, Wolfram's classification was the first attempt to classify the rules themselves. In order of complexity the classes are:
</p>
<ul><li>Class 1: Nearly all initial patterns evolve quickly into a stable, homogeneous state. Any randomness in the initial pattern disappears.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-12&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-12-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Class 2: Nearly all initial patterns evolve quickly into stable or oscillating structures. Some of the randomness in the initial pattern may filter out, but some remains. Local changes to the initial pattern tend to remain local.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-12&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-12-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Class 3: Nearly all initial patterns evolve in a pseudo-random or chaotic manner. Any stable structures that appear are quickly destroyed by the surrounding noise. Local changes to the initial pattern tend to spread indefinitely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-12&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-12-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Class 4: Nearly all initial patterns evolve into structures that interact in complex and interesting ways, with the formation of local structures that are able to survive for long periods of time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-13&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-13-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Class 2 type stable or oscillating structures may be the eventual outcome, but the number of steps required to reach this state may be very large, even when the initial pattern is relatively simple. Local changes to the initial pattern may spread indefinitely. Wolfram has <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjectured</a> that many class 4 cellular automata, if not all, are capable of <a href="/wiki/Turing_completeness" title="Turing completeness">universal computation</a>. This has been proven for Rule 110 and Conway's Game of Life.</li></ul>
<p>These definitions are qualitative in nature and there is some room for interpretation. According to Wolfram, "...with almost any general classification scheme there are inevitably cases which get assigned to one class by one definition and another class by another definition. And so it is with cellular automata: there are occasionally rules...that show some features of one class and some of another."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-231&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-231-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There have been several attempts to classify cellular automata in formally rigorous classes, inspired by Wolfram's classification. For instance, Culik and Yu proposed three well-defined classes (and a fourth one for the automata not matching any of these), which are sometimes called Culik–Yu classes; membership in these proved <a href="/wiki/Undecidable_problem" title="Undecidable problem">undecidable</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DelormeMazoyer1998&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DelormeMazoyer1998-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Voorhees1996&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voorhees1996-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Garzon1995&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garzon1995-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Wolfram's class 2 can be partitioned into two subgroups of stable (fixed-point) and oscillating (periodic) rules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-li-packard&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-li-packard-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that there are 4 classes of dynamical system came originally from Nobel-prize winning chemist <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Prigogine" title="Ilya Prigogine">Ilya Prigogine</a> who identified these 4 classes of thermodynamical systems: (1) systems in thermodynamic equilibrium, (2) spatially/temporally uniform systems, (3) chaotic systems, and (4) complex far-from-equilibrium systems with dissipative structures (see figure 1 in the 1974 paper of Nicolis, Prigogine's student).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nicolis&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nicolis-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Reversible">Reversible</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Reversible"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reversible_cellular_automaton" title="Reversible cellular automaton">Reversible cellular automaton</a></div>
<p>A cellular automaton is <i>reversible</i> if, for every current configuration of the cellular automaton, there is exactly one past configuration (<a href="/wiki/Preimage" class="mw-redirect" title="Preimage">preimage</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kari-379&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kari-379-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If one thinks of a cellular automaton as a function mapping configurations to configurations, reversibility implies that this function is <a href="/wiki/Bijective" class="mw-redirect" title="Bijective">bijective</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kari-379&#95;40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kari-379-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If a cellular automaton is reversible, its time-reversed behavior can also be described as a cellular automaton; this fact is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Curtis%E2%80%93Hedlund%E2%80%93Lyndon_theorem" title="Curtis–Hedlund–Lyndon theorem">Curtis–Hedlund–Lyndon theorem</a>, a topological characterization of cellular automata.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For cellular automata in which not every configuration has a preimage, the configurations without preimages are called <i><a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden_(cellular_automaton)" title="Garden of Eden (cellular automaton)">Garden of Eden</a></i> patterns.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schiff-103&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schiff-103-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For one-dimensional cellular automata there are known algorithms for deciding whether a rule is reversible or irreversible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, for cellular automata of two or more dimensions reversibility is <a href="/wiki/Undecidable_problem" title="Undecidable problem">undecidable</a>; that is, there is no algorithm that takes as input an automaton rule and is guaranteed to determine correctly whether the automaton is reversible. The proof by <a href="/wiki/Jarkko_Kari" title="Jarkko Kari">Jarkko Kari</a> is related to the tiling problem by <a href="/wiki/Wang_tile" title="Wang tile">Wang tiles</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Reversible cellular automata are often used to simulate such physical phenomena as gas and fluid dynamics, since they obey the laws of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>. Such cellular automata have rules specially constructed to be reversible. Such systems have been studied by <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Toffoli" title="Tommaso Toffoli">Tommaso Toffoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Margolus" title="Norman Margolus">Norman Margolus</a> and others. Several techniques can be used to explicitly construct reversible cellular automata with known inverses. Two common ones are the <a href="/wiki/Second-order_cellular_automaton" title="Second-order cellular automaton">second-order cellular automaton</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Block_cellular_automaton" title="Block cellular automaton">block cellular automaton</a>, both of which involve modifying the definition of a cellular automaton in some way. Although such automata do not strictly satisfy the definition given above, it can be shown that they can be emulated by conventional cellular automata with sufficiently large neighborhoods and numbers of states, and can therefore be considered a subset of conventional cellular automata. Conversely, it has been shown that every reversible cellular automaton can be emulated by a block cellular automaton.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Totalistic">Totalistic</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Totalistic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Totalistic"></span><span class="anchor" id="Outer&#95;totalistic"></span><span class="anchor" id="Semitotalistic"></span><span class="anchor" id="Semi-totalistic"></span><span class="anchor" id="Isotropic"></span>A special class of cellular automata are <i>totalistic</i> cellular automata. The state of each cell in a totalistic cellular automaton is represented by a number (usually an integer value drawn from a finite set), and the value of a cell at time <i>t</i> depends only on the <i>sum</i> of the values of the cells in its neighborhood (possibly including the cell itself) at time&#160;<i>t</i>&#160;−&#160;1.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wolfram-60&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wolfram-60-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cadu&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cadu-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If the state of the cell at time <i>t</i> depends on both its own state and the total of its neighbors at time&#160;<i>t</i>&#160;−&#160;1 then the cellular automaton is properly called <i>outer totalistic</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cadu&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cadu-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Conway's Game of Life</a> is outer totalistic (but not totalistic), with cell values 0 and 1. Outer totalistic cellular automata with the same <a href="/wiki/Moore_neighborhood" title="Moore neighborhood">Moore neighborhood</a> structure as Life are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Life-like_cellular_automaton" title="Life-like cellular automaton">life-like cellular automata</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-eppstein-72-73&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eppstein-72-73-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More generally, an <i>isotropic</i> ruleset is one that is not necessarily outer totalistic, but still has all the reflection symmetries. In the case of a cellular automata on a square grid, the group of symmetries is <a href="/wiki/Dihedral_group_of_order_8" title="Dihedral group of order 8">D8</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Related_automata">Related automata</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Related automata"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are many possible generalizations of the cellular automaton concept.
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Frame"><a href="/wiki/File:Oscillator.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Oscillator.gif" decoding="async" width="199" height="175" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="199" data-file-height="175" /></a><figcaption>A cellular automaton based on hexagonal cells instead of squares (rule 34/2)</figcaption></figure>
<p>One way is by using something other than a rectangular (cubic, <i>etc.</i>) grid. For example, if a plane is <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling" title="Hexagonal tiling">tiled with regular hexagons</a>, those hexagons could be used as cells. In many cases the resulting cellular automata are equivalent to those with rectangular grids with specially designed neighborhoods and rules. Another variation would be to make the grid itself irregular, such as with <a href="/wiki/Penrose_tiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Penrose tiles">Penrose tiles</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Also, rules can be probabilistic rather than deterministic. Such cellular automata are called <a href="/wiki/Probabilistic_cellular_automata" class="mw-redirect" title="Probabilistic cellular automata">probabilistic cellular automata</a>. A probabilistic rule gives, for each pattern at time <i>t</i>, the probabilities that the central cell will transition to each possible state at time <i>t</i>&#160;+&#160;1. Sometimes a simpler rule is used; for example: "The rule is the Game of Life, but on each time step there is a 0.001% probability that each cell will transition to the opposite color."
</p><p>The neighborhood or rules could change over time or space. For example, initially the new state of a cell could be determined by the horizontally adjacent cells, but for the next generation the vertical cells would be used.
</p><p>In cellular automata, the new state of a cell is not affected by the new state of other cells. This could be changed so that, for instance, a 2 by 2 block of cells can be determined by itself and the cells adjacent to itself.
</p><p>There are <i><a href="/wiki/Continuous_automaton" title="Continuous automaton">continuous automata</a></i>. These are like totalistic cellular automata, but instead of the rule and states being discrete (<i>e.g.</i> a table, using states {0,1,2}), continuous functions are used, and the states become continuous (usually values in <a href="/wiki/Unit_interval" title="Unit interval">[0,1]</a>). The state of a location is a finite number of real numbers. Certain cellular automata can yield diffusion in liquid patterns in this way.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Continuous_spatial_automata" class="mw-redirect" title="Continuous spatial automata">Continuous spatial automata</a> have a continuum of locations. The state of a location is a finite number of real numbers. Time is also continuous, and the state evolves according to differential equations. One important example is <a href="/wiki/Reaction%E2%80%93diffusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Reaction–diffusion">reaction–diffusion</a> textures, differential equations proposed by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> to explain how chemical reactions could create the stripes on <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebras</a> and spots on leopards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When these are approximated by cellular automata, they often yield similar patterns. MacLennan <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.cs.utk.edu/~mclennan/contin-comp.html">[1]</a> considers continuous spatial automata as a model of computation.
</p><p>There are known examples of continuous spatial automata, which exhibit propagating phenomena analogous to gliders in the Game of Life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i>Graph rewriting automata</i> are extensions of cellular automata based on <a href="/wiki/Graph_rewriting_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph rewriting system">graph rewriting systems</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Elementary_cellular_automata">Elementary cellular automata</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Elementary cellular automata"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elementary_cellular_automaton" title="Elementary cellular automaton">Elementary cellular automaton</a></div>
<p>The simplest nontrivial cellular automaton would be one-dimensional, with two possible states per cell, and a cell's neighbors defined as the adjacent cells on either side of it. A cell and its two neighbors form a neighborhood of 3 cells, so there are 2<sup>3</sup>&#160;=&#160;8 possible patterns for a neighborhood. A rule consists of deciding, for each pattern, whether the cell will be a 1 or a 0 in the next generation. There are then 2<sup>8</sup>&#160;=&#160;256 possible rules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bialynick-9&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bialynick-9-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif/250px-One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="170" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif/500px-One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/One-d-cellular-automate-rule-30.gif 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="340" /></a><figcaption>An animation of the way the rules of a 1D cellular automaton determine the next generation</figcaption></figure>
<p>These 256 cellular automata are generally referred to by their <a href="/wiki/Wolfram_code" title="Wolfram code">Wolfram code</a>, a standard naming convention invented by Wolfram that gives each rule a number from 0 to 255. A number of papers have analyzed and compared the distinct cases among the 256 cellular automata (many are trivially isomorphic). The <a href="/wiki/Rule_30" title="Rule 30">rule 30</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rule_90" title="Rule 90">rule 90</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rule_110" title="Rule 110">rule 110</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rule_184" title="Rule 184">rule 184</a> cellular automata are particularly interesting. The images below show the history of rules 30 and 110 when the starting configuration consists of a 1 (at the top of each image) surrounded by 0s. Each row of pixels represents a generation in the history of the automaton, with <i>t</i>=0 being the top row. Each pixel is colored white for 0 and black for&#160;1.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CA_rule30s.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/CA_rule30s.png/250px-CA_rule30s.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="125" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/CA_rule30s.png/500px-CA_rule30s.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/CA_rule30s.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="250" /></a><figcaption>Rule 30</figcaption></figure>

<p>Rule 30 exhibits <i>class 3</i> behavior, meaning even simple input patterns such as that shown lead to chaotic, seemingly random histories.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sample_run_of_Rule_110_elementary_cellular_automaton,_starting_from_single_cell.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Sample_run_of_Rule_110_elementary_cellular_automaton%2C_starting_from_single_cell.png/250px-Sample_run_of_Rule_110_elementary_cellular_automaton%2C_starting_from_single_cell.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Sample_run_of_Rule_110_elementary_cellular_automaton%2C_starting_from_single_cell.png/500px-Sample_run_of_Rule_110_elementary_cellular_automaton%2C_starting_from_single_cell.png 1.5x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="768" /></a><figcaption>256 iterations of Rule 110</figcaption></figure>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">
<caption><a href="/wiki/Rule_110" title="Rule 110">Rule 110</a> cellular automaton<br />(binary 01101110 = decimal 110)
</caption>
