<td>0
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Rule 110, like the Game of Life, exhibits what Wolfram calls <i>class 4</i> behavior, which is neither completely random nor completely repetitive. Localized structures appear and interact in various complicated-looking ways. In the course of the development of <i><a href="/wiki/A_New_Kind_of_Science" title="A New Kind of Science">A New Kind of Science</a></i>, as a research assistant to Wolfram in 1994, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Cook" title="Matthew Cook">Matthew Cook</a> proved that some of these structures were rich enough to support <a href="/wiki/Universal_Turing_machine" title="Universal Turing machine">universality</a>. This result is interesting because rule 110 is an extremely simple one-dimensional system, and difficult to engineer to perform specific behavior. This result therefore provides significant support for Wolfram's view that class 4 systems are inherently likely to be universal. Cook presented his proof at a <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_Institute" title="Santa Fe Institute">Santa Fe Institute</a> conference on Cellular Automata in 1998, but Wolfram blocked the proof from being included in the conference proceedings, as Wolfram did not want the proof announced before the publication of <i>A New Kind of Science</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-giles&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giles-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2004, Cook's proof was finally published in Wolfram's journal <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_Systems_(journal)" title="Complex Systems (journal)">Complex Systems</a></i> (Vol. 15, No. 1), over ten years after Cook came up with it. Rule 110 has been the basis for some of the smallest universal Turing machines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Rule_space">Rule space</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rule space"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An elementary cellular automaton rule is specified by 8 bits, and all elementary cellular automaton rules can be considered to sit on the <a href="/wiki/Vertex_(geometry)" title="Vertex (geometry)">vertices</a> of the 8-dimensional unit <a href="/wiki/Hypercube" title="Hypercube">hypercube</a>. This unit hypercube is the cellular automaton rule space. For next-nearest-neighbor cellular automata, a rule is specified by 2<sup>5</sup>&#160;=&#160;32 bits, and the cellular automaton rule space is a 32-dimensional unit hypercube. A distance between two rules can be defined by the number of steps required to move from one vertex, which represents the first rule, and another vertex, representing another rule, along the <a href="/wiki/Edge_(geometry)" title="Edge (geometry)">edge</a> of the hypercube. This rule-to-rule distance is also called the <a href="/wiki/Hamming_distance" title="Hamming distance">Hamming distance</a>.
</p><p>Cellular automaton rule space allows us to ask the question concerning whether rules with similar dynamical behavior are "close" to each other. Graphically drawing a high dimensional hypercube on the 2-dimensional plane remains a difficult task, and one crude locator of a rule in the hypercube is the number of bit-1 in the 8-bit string for elementary rules (or 32-bit string for the next-nearest-neighbor rules). Drawing the rules in different Wolfram classes in these slices of the rule space show that class 1 rules tend to have lower number of bit-1s, thus located in one region of the space, whereas class 3 rules tend to have higher proportion (50%) of bit-1s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-li-packard&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-li-packard-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For larger cellular automaton rule space, it is shown that class 4 rules are located between the class 1 and class 3 rules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This observation is the foundation for the phrase <a href="/wiki/Edge_of_chaos" title="Edge of chaos">edge of chaos</a>, and is reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biology">Biology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Textile_cone.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Textile_cone.JPG/250px-Textile_cone.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Textile_cone.JPG/500px-Textile_cone.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Conus_textile" title="Conus textile">Conus textile</a></i> exhibits a cellular automaton pattern on its shell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-coombs&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coombs-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">Patterns in nature</a></div>
<p>Several biological processes or phenomena can be simulated using cellular automata, which is regarded as a specific type of <a href="/wiki/Agent-based_model" title="Agent-based model">agent-based model</a> in such application contexts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In these biological simulations, it is common for the cells of the cellular automaton to be identified with biological cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some examples of biological phenomena modeled by cellular automata with a simple state space are:
</p>
<ul><li>Patterns of some <a href="/wiki/Seashell" title="Seashell">seashells</a>, like the ones in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Cone_snail" title="Cone snail">Conus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cymbiola" title="Cymbiola">Cymbiola</a></i>, are generated by natural cellular automata. The <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> cells reside in a narrow band along the shell's lip. Each cell <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secretes</a> pigments according to the activating and inhibiting activity of its neighbor pigment cells, obeying a natural version of a mathematical rule.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-coombs&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coombs-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cell band leaves the colored pattern on the shell as it grows slowly. For example, the widespread species <i><a href="/wiki/Conus_textile" title="Conus textile">Conus textile</a></i> bears a pattern resembling Wolfram's <a href="/wiki/Rule_30" title="Rule 30">rule 30</a> cellular automaton.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-coombs&#95;60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coombs-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Plants regulate their intake and loss of gases via a cellular automaton mechanism. Each <a href="/wiki/Stomata" class="mw-redirect" title="Stomata">stoma</a> on the leaf acts as a cell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Moving wave patterns on the skin of <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> can be simulated with a two-state, two-dimensional cellular automata, each state corresponding to either an expanded or retracted <a href="/wiki/Chromatophore" title="Chromatophore">chromatophore</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Threshold automata have been invented to simulate <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, and complex behaviors such as recognition and learning can be simulated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ilachinski-275&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilachinski-275-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fibroblast" title="Fibroblast">Fibroblasts</a> bear similarities to cellular automata, as each fibroblast only interacts with its neighbors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Additionally, biological phenomena which require explicit modeling of the agents' velocities (for example, those involved in <a href="/wiki/Collective_cell_migration" title="Collective cell migration">collective cell migration</a>) may be modeled by cellular automata with a more complex state space and rules, such as <a href="/wiki/BIO-LGCA" title="BIO-LGCA">biological lattice-gas cellular automata</a>. These include phenomena of great medical importance, such as:
</p>
<ul><li>Characterization of different modes of <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastatic</a> invasion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The role of <a href="/wiki/Tumour_heterogeneity" title="Tumour heterogeneity">heterogeneity</a> in the development of aggressive carcinomas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_switching" title="Phenotypic switching">Phenotypic switching</a> during tumor proliferation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chemistry">Chemistry</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chemistry"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Belousov%E2%80%93Zhabotinsky_reaction" title="Belousov–Zhabotinsky reaction">Belousov–Zhabotinsky reaction</a> is a spatio-temporal <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oscillator" title="Chemical oscillator">chemical oscillator</a> that can be simulated by means of a cellular automaton. In the 1950s <a href="/wiki/Anatol_Zhabotinsky" title="Anatol Zhabotinsky">A. M. Zhabotinsky</a> (extending the work of <a href="/wiki/B._P._Belousov" class="mw-redirect" title="B. P. Belousov">B. P. Belousov</a>) discovered that when a thin, homogenous layer of a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Malonic_acid" title="Malonic acid">malonic acid</a>, acidified bromate, and a ceric salt were mixed together and left undisturbed, fascinating geometric patterns such as concentric circles and spirals propagate across the medium. In the "Computer Recreations" section of the August 1988 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/A._K._Dewdney" title="A. K. Dewdney">A. K. Dewdney</a> discussed a cellular automaton<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> developed by Martin Gerhardt and Heike Schuster of the University of Bielefeld (Germany). This automaton produces wave patterns that resemble those in the Belousov-Zhabotinsky reaction. Combining the attachment to one only particle from the growing aggregate, following the seminal model of Witten and Sander <sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to simulate the diffusion-limited growth with the attachment to kink positions as proposed yet by Kossel and Stranski in 1920's, see <sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> for the kinetics-limited version of the attachment, Goranova et al.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> proposed a model for electrochemical co-deposition of two metal cations.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Physics">Physics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Physics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_velocity.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Gas_velocity.gif/250px-Gas_velocity.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Gas_velocity.gif/500px-Gas_velocity.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Gas_velocity.gif 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a><figcaption>Visualization of a lattice gas automaton. The shades of grey of the individual pixels are proportional to the gas particle density (between 0 and 4) at that pixel. The gas is surrounded by a shell of black cells that act as reflectors to create a closed space.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Probabilistic cellular automata are used in <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical physics">statistical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">condensed matter physics</a> to study phenomena like fluid dynamics and phase transitions. The <a href="/wiki/Ising_model" title="Ising model">Ising model</a> is a prototypical example, in which each cell can be in either of two states called "up" and "down", making an idealized representation of a <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnet</a>. By adjusting the parameters of the model, the proportion of cells being in the same state can be varied, in ways that help explicate how <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnet" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferromagnet">ferromagnets</a> become demagnetized when heated. Moreover, results from studying the demagnetization phase transition can be transferred to other phase transitions, like the evaporation of a liquid into a gas; this convenient cross-applicability is known as <a href="/wiki/Universality_(dynamical_systems)" title="Universality (dynamical systems)">universality</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The phase transition in the <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_critical_Ising_model" title="Two-dimensional critical Ising model">two-dimensional Ising model</a> and other systems in its <a href="/wiki/Universality_class" title="Universality class">universality class</a> has been of particular interest, as it requires <a href="/wiki/Conformal_field_theory" title="Conformal field theory">conformal field theory</a> to understand in depth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other cellular automata that have been of significance in physics include <a href="/wiki/Lattice_gas_automaton" title="Lattice gas automaton">lattice gas automata</a>, which simulate fluid flows. In a series of works<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krzyzewski2017&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krzyzewski2017-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Toktarbaiuly2018&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toktarbaiuly2018-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krzyzewski2019&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krzyzewski2019-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Popova2020&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Popova2020-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the so-called <i>vicinal Cellular Automaton</i> (vicCA) was proposed and further developed to model the possibly unstable growth and sublimation of vicinal crystal surfaces in 1+1D. Besides the attachment/detachment events being encoded in the rules of the CA, the adatoms on top of the vicinal form a thin layer, and their thermal motion is modeled by a Monte Carlo module.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krzyzewski2017&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krzyzewski2017-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krzyzewski2019&#95;81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krzyzewski2019-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A decisive step further was the transition of the model to 2+1D,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zaluska2021&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaluska2021-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where a number of different structures were obtained, referred to by the authors as "vicinal creatures"—step bunches, step meanders, nano-pillars, nanowires, etc.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zaluska2021&#95;83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaluska2021-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The vicCA model was extensively used by Alexey Redkov<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Redkov2025&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redkov2025-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to develop a Machine Learning algorithm on top of it, significantly speeding up calculations by a factor of 10<sup>5</sup> while enabling systematic classification of the observed phenomena.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Computer_science,_coding,_and_communication"><span id="Computer_science.2C_coding.2C_and_communication"></span>Computer science, coding, and communication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Computer science, coding, and communication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Cellular automaton processors are physical implementations of CA concepts, which can process information computationally. Processing elements are arranged in a regular grid of identical cells. The grid is usually a square tiling, or <a href="/wiki/Tessellation" title="Tessellation">tessellation</a>, of two or three dimensions; other tilings are possible, but not yet used. Cell states are determined only by interactions with adjacent neighbor cells. No means exists to communicate directly with cells farther away.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One such cellular automaton processor array configuration is the <a href="/wiki/Systolic_array" title="Systolic array">systolic array</a>. Cell interaction can be via electric charge, magnetism, vibration (<a href="/wiki/Phonons" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonons">phonons</a> at quantum scales), or any other physically useful means. This can be done in several ways so that no wires are needed between any elements. This is very unlike processors used in most computers today (<a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_architecture" title="Von Neumann architecture">von Neumann designs</a>) which are divided into sections with elements that can communicate with distant elements over wires.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rule_30" title="Rule 30">Rule 30</a> was originally suggested as a possible <a href="/wiki/Block_cipher" title="Block cipher">block cipher</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a>. Two-dimensional cellular automata can be used for constructing a <a href="/wiki/Pseudorandom_number_generator" title="Pseudorandom number generator">pseudorandom number generator</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cellular automata have been proposed for <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">public-key cryptography</a>. The <a href="/wiki/One-way_function" title="One-way function">one-way function</a> is the evolution of a finite CA whose inverse is believed to be hard to find. Given the rule, anyone can easily calculate future states, but it appears to be very difficult to calculate previous states.  Cellular automata have also been applied to design <a href="/wiki/ECC_memory" title="ECC memory">error correction codes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other problems that can be solved with cellular automata include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Firing_squad_synchronization_problem" title="Firing squad synchronization problem">Firing squad synchronization problem</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Majority_problem_(cellular_automaton)" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority problem (cellular automaton)">Majority problem</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Generative_art_and_music">Generative art and music</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Generative art and music"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Generative_art" title="Generative art">Generative art</a></div>
<p>Cellular automata have been used in <a href="/wiki/Generative_music" title="Generative music">generative music</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_music" title="Evolutionary music">evolutionary music</a> composition<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Procedural_terrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Procedural terrain">procedural terrain</a> generation in video games.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Maze_generation">Maze generation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Maze generation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Maze_generation_algorithm#Cellular_automaton_algorithms" title="Maze generation algorithm">Maze generation algorithm § Cellular automaton algorithms</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Maze_generation_algorithm&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cellular_automata" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellular automata">cellular automata</a> can be used to generate mazes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maze&#95;generation&#95;algorithm&#95;ca&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maze_generation_algorithm_ca-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two well-known such cellular automata, Maze and Mazectric, have rulestrings B3/S12345 and B3/S1234.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maze&#95;generation&#95;algorithm&#95;ca&#95;91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maze_generation_algorithm_ca-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the former, this means that cells survive from one generation to the next if they have at least one and at most five <a href="/wiki/Moore_neighbourhood" class="mw-redirect" title="Moore neighbourhood">neighbours</a>. In the latter, this means that cells survive if they have one to four neighbours. If a cell has exactly three neighbours, it is born. It is similar to <a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_Game_of_Life" title="Conway&#39;s Game of Life">Conway's Game of Life</a> in that patterns that do not have a living cell adjacent to 1, 4, or 5 other living cells in any generation will behave identically to it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maze&#95;generation&#95;algorithm&#95;ca&#95;91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maze_generation_algorithm_ca-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, for large patterns, it behaves very differently from Life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maze&#95;generation&#95;algorithm&#95;ca&#95;91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maze_generation_algorithm_ca-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For a random starting pattern, these maze-generating cellular automata will evolve into complex mazes with well-defined walls outlining corridors. Mazecetric, which has the rule B3/S1234 has a tendency to generate longer and straighter corridors compared with Maze, with the rule B3/S12345.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maze&#95;generation&#95;algorithm&#95;ca&#95;91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maze_generation_algorithm_ca-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since these cellular automaton rules are <a href="/wiki/Deterministic" class="mw-redirect" title="Deterministic">deterministic</a>, each maze generated is uniquely determined by its random starting pattern. This is a significant drawback since the mazes tend to be relatively predictable.
</p><p>Like some of the graph-theory based methods described above, these cellular automata typically generate mazes from a single starting pattern; hence it will usually be relatively easy to find the way to the starting cell, but harder to find the way anywhere else.
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Specific_rules">Specific rules</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cellular_automaton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Specific rules"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Specific cellular automata rules include:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1184024115">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brian%27s_Brain" title="Brian&#39;s Brain">Brian's Brain</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codd%27s_cellular_automaton" title="Codd&#39;s cellular automaton">Codd's cellular automaton</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CoDi" title="CoDi">CoDi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conway%27s_game_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Conway&#39;s game of life">Conway's game of life</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Day_and_Night_(cellular_automaton)" title="Day and Night (cellular automaton)">Day and Night</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Langton%27s_ant" title="Langton&#39;s ant">Langton's ant</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Langton%27s_loops" title="Langton&#39;s loops">Langton's loops</a></li>

<li id="cite&#95;note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The phrase "life-like cellular automaton" dates back at least to <a href="#CITEREFBarralChatéManneville1992">Barral, Chaté &amp; Manneville (1992)</a>, who used it in a broader sense to refer to outer totalistic automata, not necessarily of two dimensions. The more specific meaning given here was used e.g. in several chapters of <a href="#CITEREFAdamatzky2010">Adamatzky (2010)</a>. See: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFBarralChatéManneville1992" class="citation journal cs1">Barral, Bernard; Chaté, Hugues; Manneville, Paul (1992). "Collective behaviors in a family of high-dimensional cellular automata". <i>Physics Letters A</i>. <b>163</b> (4): <span class="nowrap">279–</span>285. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1992PhLA..163..279B">1992PhLA..163..279B</a>. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2F0375-9601%2892%2991013-H">10.1016/0375-9601(92)91013-H</a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Physics+Letters+A&amp;rft.atitle=Collective+behaviors+in+a+family+of+high-dimensional+cellular+automata&amp;rft.volume=163&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=279-285&amp;rft.date=1992&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2F0375-9601%2892%2991013-H&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Abibcode%2F1992PhLA..163..279B&amp;rft.aulast=Barral&amp;rft.aufirst=Bernard&amp;rft.au=Chat%C3%A9%2C+Hugues&amp;rft.au=Manneville%2C+Paul&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACellular+automaton" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-eppstein-72-73-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-eppstein-72-73_52-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEppstein2010">Eppstein 2010</a>, pp.&#160;72–73</span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1314755338" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1311551236" /><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" />
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/60px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/120px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikibooks has a book on the topic of: <i><b><a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cellular_Automata" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cellular Automata">Cellular Automata</a></b></i></div></div>
</div>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://mcell.ca/">Mirek's Cellebration</a> – Home to free cellular automata explorer software, "MCell". The software supports a large number of 1D and 2D rules. The site provides both an extensive rules lexicon and many image galleries loaded with examples of rules. Source code (JavaScript) is available.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.sourceforge.net/projects/golly">Golly</a> supports von Neumann, Nobili, GOL, and a great many other systems of cellular automata.  Developed by Tomas Rokicki and Andrew Trevorrow.  This is the only simulator currently available that can demonstrate von Neumann type self-replication.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://atlas.wolfram.com/TOC/TOC_200.html">Wolfram Atlas</a> – An atlas of various types of one-dimensional cellular automata.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.conwaylife.com/">Conway Life</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://cafaq.com/">Cellular automaton FAQ</a> from the newsgroup comp.theory.cell-automata</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://cell-auto.com/neighbourhood/index.html">"Neighbourhood Survey"</a> (includes discussion on triangular grids, and larger neighborhood CAs)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bactra.org/notebooks/cellular-automata.html">Cosma Shalizi's Cellular Automata Notebook</a> contains an extensive list of academic and professional reference material.</li></ul>
